      <h1 id="section_0">Bitcoin</h1>
     </div>
     <div id="bodyContent" class="content">
      <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
       <script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script>
       <div class="mw-parser-output">
        <div class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0">
         <p><b>Bitcoin</b> is a worldwide <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency" title="Cryptocurrency">cryptocurrency</a> and digital <a href="/wiki/Payment_system" title="Payment system">payment system</a><sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:3</sup> called the first decentralized <a href="/wiki/Digital_currency" title="Digital currency">digital currency</a>, as the system works without a central repository or single administrator.<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Reuters101_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters101-18">[14]</a></sup> It was invented by an unknown programmer, or a group of programmers, under the name <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a><sup id="cite_ref-whoissn_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoissn-19">[15]</a></sup> and released as <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source software</a> in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-NY2011_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY2011-20">[16]</a></sup> The system is <a href="/wiki/Peer-to-peer" title="Peer-to-peer">peer-to-peer</a>, and transactions take place between users directly, without an intermediary.<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4</sup> These transactions are verified by network <a href="/wiki/Node_(networking)" title="Node (networking)">nodes</a> and recorded in a public <a href="/wiki/Distributed_ledger" title="Distributed ledger">distributed ledger</a> called a <i><a href="#Blockchain">blockchain</a></i>.</p>

            <th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Monetary_inflation" title="Monetary inflation">Supply growth</a></th> 
            <td>12.5 bitcoins per block (approximately every ten minutes) until mid 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-quantitative_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quantitative-16">[12]</a></sup> and then afterwards 6.25 bitcoins per block for 4 years until next halving. This halving continues until 2110–40, when 21 million bitcoins will have been issued.</td>

         <p>Besides being created as a reward for <a href="#Mining">mining</a>, bitcoin can be exchanged for other currencies,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> products, and services. As of February 2015, over 100,000 merchants and vendors accepted bitcoin as payment.<sup id="cite_ref-100tmerchants_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100tmerchants-22">[18]</a></sup> Bitcoin can also be held as an investment. According to research produced by <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> in 2017, there are 2.9 to 5.8 million unique users using a cryptocurrency wallet, most of them using bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-CU2017_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CU2017-23">[19]</a></sup></p> 
         <div class="toclimit-3"></div> 
         <div id="toc" class="toc-mobile">
          <h2>Contents</h2>
         </div>
        </div>
        <h2 class="in-block section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)">
         <div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h2>
        <div class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1"> 
         <p>The word <i>bitcoin</i> occurred in the <a href="/wiki/White_paper" title="White paper">white paper</a><sup id="cite_ref-paper_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paper-24">[20]</a></sup> that defined bitcoin published on 31 October 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-ageofcr_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ageofcr-25">[21]</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compound</a> of the words <i><a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bit</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coin</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-btox_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btox-26">[22]</a></sup> The white paper frequently uses the shorter <i>coin</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-paper_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paper-24">[20]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>There is no uniform convention for <i>bitcoin</i> capitalization. Some sources use <i>Bitcoin</i>, capitalized, to refer to the technology and <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">network</a> and <i>bitcoin</i>, lowercase, to refer to the unit of account.<sup id="cite_ref-capitalization_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capitalization-27">[23]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chronicle_of_Higher_Education" title="The Chronicle of Higher Education">The Chronicle of Higher Education</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-btox_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btox-26">[22]</a></sup> advocate use of lowercase <i>bitcoin</i> in all cases, a convention which this article follows.</p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Units">Units</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Units" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>The unit of account of the bitcoin system is bitcoin. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, symbols used to represent bitcoin are BTC,<sup id="cite_ref-BTCcode_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTCcode-4">[note 1]</a></sup> XBT,<sup id="cite_ref-XBTcode_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-XBTcode-8">[note 2]</a></sup> and ? (U+20BF, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:BitcoinSign.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="BitcoinSign.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/BitcoinSign.svg/9px-BitcoinSign.svg.png" width="9" height="12" data-file-width="274" data-file-height="360">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 9px;height: 12px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/BitcoinSign.svg/9px-BitcoinSign.svg.png" data-alt="BitcoinSign.svg" data-width="9" data-height="12" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/BitcoinSign.svg/14px-BitcoinSign.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/BitcoinSign.svg/18px-BitcoinSign.svg.png 2x">&nbsp;</span></a></span>).<sup id="cite_ref-BTCsym_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTCsym-10">[note 3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-btcregs_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-btcregs-30">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:2</sup> Small amounts of bitcoin used as alternative units are millibitcoin (mBTC),<sup id="cite_ref-mBTC_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mBTC-2">[1]</a></sup> microbitcoin (µBTC, sometimes referred to as <i>bit</i>), and satoshi. Named in homage to bitcoin's creator, a <i>satoshi</i> is the smallest amount within bitcoin representing 0.00000001 bitcoin, one hundred millionth of a bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-satoshi_unit_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-satoshi_unit-13">[9]</a></sup> A <i>millibitcoin</i> equals to 0.001 bitcoin, one thousandth of a bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> One <i>microbitcoin</i> equals to 0.000001 bitcoin, one millionth of a bitcoin.</p> 
        </div>
        <h2 class="in-block section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)">
         <div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Design" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h2>
        <div class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2"> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Blockchain">Blockchain</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Blockchain" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
          For a broader coverage related to this topic, see 
          <a href="/wiki/Blockchain" title="Blockchain">Blockchain</a>.
         </div> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Utxo-count.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Utxo-count.svg/220px-Utxo-count.svg.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="319">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Utxo-count.svg/220px-Utxo-count.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Utxo-count.svg/330px-Utxo-count.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Utxo-count.svg/440px-Utxo-count.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Utxo-count.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Number of unspent transaction outputs
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>The <i>blockchain</i> is a public <a href="/wiki/Ledger" title="Ledger">ledger</a> that records bitcoin transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-econbc_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econbc-32">[28]</a></sup> A novel solution accomplishes this without any trusted central authority: the maintenance of the blockchain is performed by a <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">network</a> of communicating <a href="/wiki/Node_(networking)" title="Node (networking)">nodes</a> running bitcoin software.<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup> Transactions of the form <i>payer X sends Y bitcoins to payee Z</i> are <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting_(networking)" title="Broadcasting (networking)">broadcast</a> to this network using readily available software applications.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup> Network nodes can validate transactions, add them to their copy of the ledger, and then broadcast these ledger additions to other nodes. The blockchain is a <a href="/wiki/Distributed_database" title="Distributed database">distributed database</a> – to achieve independent verification of the chain of ownership of any and every bitcoin amount, each network node stores its own copy of the blockchain.<sup id="cite_ref-f_c2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-f_c2-34">[30]</a></sup> Approximately six times per hour, a new group of accepted transactions, a block, is created, added to the blockchain, and quickly published to all nodes. This allows bitcoin software to determine when a particular bitcoin amount has been spent, which is necessary in order to prevent <a href="/wiki/Double-spending" title="Double-spending">double-spending</a> in an environment without central oversight. Whereas a conventional ledger records the transfers of actual <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">bills</a> or <a href="/wiki/Promissory_note" title="Promissory note">promissory notes</a> that exist apart from it, the blockchain is the only place that bitcoins can be said to exist in the form of unspent outputs of transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 5</sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Transactions">Transactions</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Transactions" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
          See also: 
          <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_network" title="Bitcoin network">Bitcoin network</a>
         </div> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:BTC_number_of_transactions_per_month.png" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/BTC_number_of_transactions_per_month.png/220px-BTC_number_of_transactions_per_month.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="756" data-file-height="425">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/BTC_number_of_transactions_per_month.png/220px-BTC_number_of_transactions_per_month.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:BTC_number_of_transactions_per_month.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Number of bitcoin transactions per month (logarithmic scale)
            <sup id="cite_ref-Blockchain.info_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blockchain.info-35">[31]</a></sup>
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>Transactions are defined using a <a href="/wiki/Forth_(programming_language)" title="Forth (programming language)">Forth</a>-like scripting language.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:ch. 5</sup> A valid transaction must have one or more inputs.<sup id="cite_ref-EconOfBTC_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconOfBTC-36">[32]</a></sup> Every input must be an unspent output of a previous transaction. The transaction must carry the digital signature of every input owner. The use of multiple inputs corresponds to the use of multiple coins in a cash transaction. A transaction can also have multiple outputs, allowing one to make multiple payments in one go. A transaction output can be specified as an arbitrary multiple of satoshi. As in a cash transaction, the sum of inputs (coins used to pay) can exceed the intended sum of payments. In such a case, an additional output is used, returning the change back to the payer.<sup id="cite_ref-EconOfBTC_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconOfBTC-36">[32]</a></sup> Any input satoshis not accounted for in the transaction outputs become the transaction fee.<sup id="cite_ref-EconOfBTC_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconOfBTC-36">[32]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Transaction_fees">Transaction fees</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Transaction fees" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:An_actual_Bitcoin_transaction_from_the_Kraken_cryptocurrency_exchange_to_a_hardware_LedgerWallet.jpg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/An_actual_Bitcoin_transaction_from_the_Kraken_cryptocurrency_exchange_to_a_hardware_LedgerWallet.jpg/220px-An_actual_Bitcoin_transaction_from_the_Kraken_cryptocurrency_exchange_to_a_hardware_LedgerWallet.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3065" data-file-height="2349">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 169px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/An_actual_Bitcoin_transaction_from_the_Kraken_cryptocurrency_exchange_to_a_hardware_LedgerWallet.jpg/220px-An_actual_Bitcoin_transaction_from_the_Kraken_cryptocurrency_exchange_to_a_hardware_LedgerWallet.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="169" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:An_actual_Bitcoin_transaction_from_the_Kraken_cryptocurrency_exchange_to_a_hardware_LedgerWallet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> An actual bitcoin transaction including the fee from a webbased cryptocurrency exchange to a hardware wallet.
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>Paying a transaction fee is optional.<sup id="cite_ref-EconOfBTC_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconOfBTC-36">[32]</a></sup> Miners can choose which transactions to process<sup id="cite_ref-EconOfBTC_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EconOfBTC-36">[32]</a></sup> and prioritize those that pay higher fees. Fees are based on the storage size of the transaction generated, which in turn is dependent on the number of inputs used to create the transaction. Furthermore, priority is given to older unspent inputs.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 8</sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Mining">Mining</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mining" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg/220px-History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="319">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg/220px-History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg/330px-History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg/440px-History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Bitcoin_difficulty.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> 
            <a href="/wiki/Semi-log_plot" title="Semi-log plot">Semi-log plot</a> of relative mining difficulty.
            <sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[note 4]</a></sup>
            <sup id="cite_ref-Blockchain.info_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blockchain.info-35">[31]</a></sup>
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p><i>Mining</i> is a record-keeping service.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[note 5]</a></sup> Miners keep the blockchain consistent, complete, and unalterable by repeatedly verifying and collecting newly broadcast transactions into a new group of transactions called a <i>block</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-econbc_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econbc-32">[28]</a></sup> Each block contains a <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_hash" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptographic hash">cryptographic hash</a> of the previous block,<sup id="cite_ref-econbc_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econbc-32">[28]</a></sup> using the <a href="/wiki/SHA-256" class="mw-redirect" title="SHA-256">SHA-256</a> hashing algorithm,<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 7</sup> which links it to the previous block,<sup id="cite_ref-econbc_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econbc-32">[28]</a></sup> thus giving the blockchain its name.</p> 
         <p>In order to be accepted by the rest of the network, a new block must contain a so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Proof-of-work" class="mw-redirect" title="Proof-of-work">proof-of-work</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-econbc_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econbc-32">[28]</a></sup> The proof-of-work requires miners to find a number called a <i><a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_nonce" title="Cryptographic nonce">nonce</a></i>, such that when the block content is <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic_hash" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptographic hash">hashed</a> along with the nonce, the result is numerically smaller than the network's <i>difficulty target</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 8</sup> This proof is easy for any node in the network to verify, but extremely time-consuming to generate, as for a secure cryptographic hash, miners must try many different nonce values (usually the sequence of tested values is 0, 1, 2, 3, ...<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 8</sup>) before meeting the difficulty target.</p> 
         <p>Every 2016 blocks (approximately 14 days at roughly 10 min per block), the difficulty target is adjusted based on the network's recent performance, with the aim of keeping the average time between new blocks at ten minutes. In this way the system automatically adapts to the total amount of mining power on the network.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 8</sup></p> 
         <p>Between 1 March 2014 and 1 March 2015, the average number of nonces miners had to try before creating a new block increased from 16.4 quintillion to 200.5 quintillion.<sup id="cite_ref-diffhistory_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diffhistory-40">[34]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>The proof-of-work system, alongside the chaining of blocks, makes modifications of the blockchain extremely hard, as an attacker must modify all subsequent blocks in order for the modifications of one block to be accepted.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[35]</a></sup> As new blocks are mined all the time, the difficulty of modifying a block increases as time passes and the number of subsequent blocks (also called <i>confirmations</i> of the given block) increases.<sup id="cite_ref-econbc_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econbc-32">[28]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Supply">Supply</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Supply" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Total-bitcoins.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Total-bitcoins.svg/220px-Total-bitcoins.svg.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="319">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Total-bitcoins.svg/220px-Total-bitcoins.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Total-bitcoins.svg/330px-Total-bitcoins.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Total-bitcoins.svg/440px-Total-bitcoins.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Total-bitcoins.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Total bitcoins in circulation.
            <sup id="cite_ref-Blockchain.info_35-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blockchain.info-35">[31]</a></sup>
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>The successful miner finding the new block is rewarded with newly created bitcoins and transaction fees.<sup id="cite_ref-bloombergvance111413_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloombergvance111413-42">[36]</a></sup> As of 9 July 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>,<sup id="cite_ref-BlockChain.info_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlockChain.info-43">[37]</a></sup> the reward amounted to 12.5 newly created bitcoins per block added to the blockchain. To claim the reward, a special transaction called a <i>coinbase</i> is included with the processed payments.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 8</sup> All bitcoins in existence have been created in such coinbase transactions. The <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Bitcoin protocol">bitcoin protocol</a> specifies that the reward for adding a block will be halved every 210,000 blocks (approximately every four years). Eventually, the reward will decrease to zero, and the limit of 21 million bitcoins<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[note 6]</a></sup> will be reached <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">c.</a> 2140; the record keeping will then be rewarded by transaction fees solely.<sup id="cite_ref-KWY_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KWY-45">[38]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>In other words, bitcoin's inventor Nakamoto set a <a href="/wiki/Monetary_policy" title="Monetary policy">monetary policy</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Artificial_scarcity" title="Artificial scarcity">artificial scarcity</a> at bitcoin's inception that there would only ever be 21&nbsp;million bitcoins in total. Their numbers are being released roughly every ten minutes and the rate at which they are generated would drop by half every four years until all were in <a href="/wiki/Circulation_(currency)" title="Circulation (currency)">circulation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[39]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Wallets">Wallets</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wallets" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
          Main article: 
          <a href="/wiki/Cryptocurrency_wallet" title="Cryptocurrency wallet">Cryptocurrency wallet</a>
         </div> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Electrum_Bitcoin_Wallet.png" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Electrum_Bitcoin_Wallet.png/220px-Electrum_Bitcoin_Wallet.png" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="405" data-file-height="226">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 123px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Electrum_Bitcoin_Wallet.png/220px-Electrum_Bitcoin_Wallet.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="123" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Electrum_Bitcoin_Wallet.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Electrum bitcoin wallet
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_paper_wallet_generated_at_bitaddress.jpg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Bitcoin_paper_wallet_generated_at_bitaddress.jpg/220px-Bitcoin_paper_wallet_generated_at_bitaddress.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="729" data-file-height="390">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 118px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Bitcoin_paper_wallet_generated_at_bitaddress.jpg/220px-Bitcoin_paper_wallet_generated_at_bitaddress.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="118" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_paper_wallet_generated_at_bitaddress.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Bitcoin paper wallet generated at bitaddress.org
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:10elqpi.jpg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/10elqpi.jpg/220px-10elqpi.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="900">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/10elqpi.jpg/220px-10elqpi.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:10elqpi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Trezor hardware wallet
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>A <i>wallet</i> stores the information necessary to transact bitcoins. While wallets are often described as a place to hold<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[40]</a></sup> or store bitcoins,<sup id="cite_ref-3ceos_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3ceos-48">[41]</a></sup> due to the nature of the system, bitcoins are inseparable from the blockchain transaction ledger. A better way to describe a wallet is something that "stores the digital credentials for your bitcoin holdings"<sup id="cite_ref-3ceos_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3ceos-48">[41]</a></sup> and allows one to access (and spend) them. Bitcoin uses <a href="/wiki/Public-key_cryptography" title="Public-key cryptography">public-key cryptography</a>, in which two cryptographic keys, one public and one private, are generated.<sup id="cite_ref-Economist113013Pressure_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economist113013Pressure-49">[42]</a></sup> At its most basic, a wallet is a collection of these keys.</p> 
         <p>There are several types of wallets. <i>Software wallets</i> connect to the network and allow spending bitcoins in addition to holding the credentials that prove ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-Bitcoin_Clients_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bitcoin_Clients-50">[43]</a></sup> Software wallets can be split further in two categories: full clients and lightweight clients.</p> 
         <ul>
          <li><b>Full clients</b> verify transactions directly on a local copy of the blockchain (over 110&nbsp;GB as of May 2017<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup>), or a subset of the blockchain (around 2&nbsp;GB<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup>). Because of its size and complexity, the entire blockchain is not suitable for all computing devices.</li> 
          <li><b>Lightweight clients</b> on the other hand consult a full client to send and receive transactions without requiring a local copy of the entire blockchain (see <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_network#Payment_verification" title="Bitcoin network">simplified payment verification</a> – SPV). This makes lightweight clients much faster to set up and allows them to be used on low-power, low-bandwidth devices such as smartphones. When using a lightweight wallet however, the user must trust the server to a certain degree. When using a lightweight client, the server can not steal bitcoins, but it can report faulty values back to the user. With both types of software wallets, the users are responsible for keeping their private keys in a secure place.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[46]</a></sup></li> 
         </ul>
         <p>Besides software wallets, Internet services called <i>online wallets</i> offer similar functionality but may be easier to use. In this case, credentials to access funds are stored with the online wallet provider rather than on the user's hardware.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[48]</a></sup> As a result, the user must have complete trust in the wallet provider. A malicious provider or a breach in server security may cause entrusted bitcoins to be stolen. An example of such security breach occurred with <a href="/wiki/Mt._Gox" title="Mt. Gox">Mt. Gox</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-mtgoxdetails_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mtgoxdetails-56">[49]</a></sup></p> 
         <p><i>Physical wallets</i> store the credentials necessary to spend bitcoins offline.<sup id="cite_ref-3ceos_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3ceos-48">[41]</a></sup> Examples combine a novelty coin with these credentials printed on metal.<sup id="cite_ref-theverge_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theverge-57">[50]</a></sup> Others are simply paper printouts. Another type of wallet called a <i>hardware wallet</i> keeps credentials offline while facilitating transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[51]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Reference_implementation">Reference implementation</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reference implementation" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>The first wallet program was released in 2009 by <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a> as <a href="/wiki/Open_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Open source">open-source</a> code.<sup id="cite_ref-NY2011_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY2011-20">[16]</a></sup> Sometimes referred to as the "Satoshi client", this is also known as the <a href="/wiki/Reference_implementation" title="Reference implementation">reference client</a> because it serves to define the bitcoin protocol and acts as a standard for other implementations.<sup id="cite_ref-Bitcoin_Clients_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bitcoin_Clients-50">[43]</a></sup> In version 0.5 the client moved from the <a href="/wiki/WxWidgets" title="WxWidgets">wxWidgets</a> user interface toolkit to <a href="/wiki/Qt_(software)" title="Qt (software)">Qt</a>, and the whole bundle was referred to as Bitcoin-Qt.<sup id="cite_ref-Bitcoin_Clients_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bitcoin_Clients-50">[43]</a></sup> After the release of version 0.9, the software bundle was renamed <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Core" title="Bitcoin Core">Bitcoin Core</a> to distinguish itself from the network.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[53]</a></sup> Today, other <a href="/wiki/Fork_(software_development)" title="Fork (software development)">forks</a> of Bitcoin Core exist such as <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_XT" title="Bitcoin XT">Bitcoin XT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Classic" title="Bitcoin Classic">Bitcoin Classic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Unlimited" title="Bitcoin Unlimited">Bitcoin Unlimited</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[55]</a></sup> and Parity Bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[56]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership">Ownership</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ownership" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg/220px-Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="361" data-file-height="227">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 138px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg/220px-Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="138" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg/330px-Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg/440px-Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_Transaction_Visual.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Simplified chain of ownership.
            <sup id="cite_ref-paper_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paper-24">[20]</a></sup> In reality, a transaction can have more than one input and more than one output.
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>Ownership of bitcoins implies that a user can spend bitcoins associated with a specific address. To do so, a payer must <a href="/wiki/Digital_signature" title="Digital signature">digitally sign</a> the transaction using the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Private_key" class="mw-redirect" title="Private key">private key</a>. Without knowledge of the private key, the transaction cannot be signed and bitcoins cannot be spent. The network verifies the signature using the <a href="/wiki/Public_key" class="mw-redirect" title="Public key">public key</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos2014_11-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos2014-11">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:ch. 5</sup></p> 
         <p>If the private key is lost, the <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_network" title="Bitcoin network">bitcoin network</a> will not recognize any other evidence of ownership;<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup> the coins are then unusable, and effectively lost. For example, in 2013 one user claimed to have lost 7,500 bitcoins, worth $7.5 million at the time, when he accidentally discarded a hard drive containing his private key.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup> A backup of his key(s) would have prevented this.<sup id="cite_ref-backup_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-backup-65">[58]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Decentralization">Decentralization</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Decentralization" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Bitcoin creator Satoshi Nakamoto designed bitcoin not to need a central authority.<sup id="cite_ref-paper_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paper-24">[20]</a></sup> Per sources such as the academic Mercatus Center,<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup> U.S. Treasury,<sup id="cite_ref-JSC_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSC-14">[10]</a></sup> <i>Reuters</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters101_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters101-18">[14]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-USoff_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USoff-66">[59]</a></sup> <i>The Daily Herald</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-dh150613_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dh150613-67">[60]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-kopstein_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kopstein-68">[61]</a></sup> and others, bitcoin is decentralized.</p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Privacy">Privacy</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Privacy" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Bitcoin is <a href="/wiki/Pseudonymous" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudonymous">pseudonymous</a>, meaning that funds are not tied to real-world entities but rather bitcoin addresses. Owners of bitcoin addresses are not explicitly identified, but all transactions on the blockchain are public. In addition, transactions can be linked to individuals and companies through "idioms of use" (e.g., transactions that spend coins from multiple inputs indicate that the inputs may have a common owner) and corroborating public transaction data with known information on owners of certain addresses.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[62]</a></sup> Additionally, bitcoin exchanges, where bitcoins are traded for traditional currencies, may be required by law to collect personal information.<sup id="cite_ref-5facts_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5facts-70">[63]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>To heighten financial privacy, a new bitcoin address can be generated for each transaction.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup> For example, hierarchical deterministic wallets generate <a href="/wiki/Pseudorandom" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudorandom">pseudorandom</a> "rolling addresses" for every transaction from a single <a href="/wiki/Random_seed" title="Random seed">seed</a>, while only requiring a single passphrase to be remembered to recover all corresponding private keys.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> Additionally, "mixing" and <a href="/wiki/CoinJoin" title="CoinJoin">CoinJoin</a> services aggregate multiple users' coins and output them to fresh addresses to increase privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> Researchers at <a href="/wiki/Stanford_University" title="Stanford University">Stanford University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concordia_University" title="Concordia University">Concordia University</a> have also shown that bitcoin exchanges and other entities can prove assets, <a href="/wiki/Liability_(financial_accounting)" title="Liability (financial accounting)">liabilities</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solvency" title="Solvency">solvency</a> without revealing their addresses using <a href="/wiki/Zero-knowledge_proof" title="Zero-knowledge proof">zero-knowledge proofs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>According to Dan Blystone, "Ultimately, bitcoin resembles cash as much as it does credit cards."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Fungibility">Fungibility</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fungibility" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Wallets and similar software technically handle all bitcoins as equivalent, establishing the basic level of <a href="/wiki/Fungibility" title="Fungibility">fungibility</a>. Researchers have pointed out that the history of each bitcoin is registered and publicly available in the blockchain ledger, and that some users may refuse to accept bitcoins coming from controversial transactions, which would harm bitcoin's fungibility.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[69]</a></sup> Projects such as <a href="/wiki/CryptoNote" title="CryptoNote">CryptoNote</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zerocoin" title="Zerocoin">Zerocoin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dark_Wallet" class="mw-redirect" title="Dark Wallet">Dark Wallet</a> aim to address these privacy and fungibility issues.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[71]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Governance" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Bitcoin was initially led by <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a>. Nakamoto stepped back in 2010 and handed the network alert key to <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Andresen" title="Gavin Andresen">Gavin Andresen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-governance_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-governance-79">[72]</a></sup> Andresen stated he subsequently sought to decentralize control stating: "As soon as Satoshi stepped back and threw the project onto my shoulders, one of the first things I did was try to decentralize that. So, if I get hit by a bus, it would be clear that the project would go on."<sup id="cite_ref-governance_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-governance-79">[72]</a></sup> This left opportunity for controversy to develop over the future development path of bitcoin. The reference implementation of the bitcoin protocol called Bitcoin Core obtained competing versions that propose to solve various governance and blocksize debates; as of August 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the alternatives were called Bitcoin XT, Bitcoin Classic, Bitcoin Unlimited<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[73]</a></sup> and BTC1.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[74]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Scalability">Scalability</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Scalability" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
          Main article: 
          <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_scalability_problem" title="Bitcoin scalability problem">Bitcoin scalability problem</a>
         </div> 
         <p>The blocks in the blockchain are limited to one <a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">megabyte</a> in size, which has created problems for bitcoin transaction processing, such as increasing transaction fees and delayed processing of transactions that cannot be fit into a block.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[75]</a></sup> Contenders to solve the scalability problem include <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Cash" title="Bitcoin Cash">Bitcoin Cash</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-split_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-split-83">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Classic" title="Bitcoin Classic">Bitcoin Classic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Unlimited" title="Bitcoin Unlimited">Bitcoin Unlimited</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-hopesto_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hopesto-85">[78]</a></sup> and SegWit2x.<sup id="cite_ref-sw2x_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sw2x-86">[79]</a></sup> On 24 August 2017 (at block 481,824) <a href="/wiki/Segregated_Witness" class="mw-redirect" title="Segregated Witness">Segregated Witness</a> went live, lowering fees and increasing block capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-CDSWLaunch_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CDSWLaunch-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="the source was published before SegWit went live; cannot confirm the claim (October 2017)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
        </div>
        <h2 class="in-block section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)">
         <div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: History" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h2>
        <div class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3"> 
         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
          Main article: 
          <a href="/wiki/History_of_bitcoin" title="History of bitcoin">History of bitcoin</a>
         </div> 
         <p>Bitcoin was created<sup id="cite_ref-NY2011_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY2011-20">[16]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Satoshi_Nakamoto" title="Satoshi Nakamoto">Satoshi Nakamoto</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-whoissn_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoissn-19">[15]</a></sup> who published the invention<sup id="cite_ref-NY2011_20-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY2011-20">[16]</a></sup> on 31 October 2008 to a cryptography mailing list<sup id="cite_ref-ageofcr_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ageofcr-25">[21]</a></sup> in a research paper called "Bitcoin: A Peer-to-Peer Electronic Cash System".<sup id="cite_ref-paper_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paper-24">[20]</a></sup> Nakamoto implemented bitcoin as <a href="/wiki/Open_source_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Open source code">open source code</a> and released in January 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-NY2011_20-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NY2011-20">[16]</a></sup> The identity of Nakamoto remains unknown, though many have claimed to know it.<sup id="cite_ref-whoissn_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoissn-19">[15]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>In January 2009, the bitcoin network came into existence with the release of the first <a href="/wiki/Open_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Open source">open source</a> bitcoin client and the issuance of the first bitcoins,<sup id="cite_ref-Wired:RFB_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired:RFB-88">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[83]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[84]</a></sup> with Satoshi Nakamoto mining the first block of bitcoins ever (known as the <i>genesis block</i>), which had a reward of 50 bitcoins.</p> 
         <p>One of the first supporters, adopters, contributor to bitcoin and receiver of the first bitcoin transaction was programmer <a href="/wiki/Hal_Finney_(cypherpunk)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hal Finney (cypherpunk)">Hal Finney</a>. Finney downloaded the bitcoin software the day it was released, and received 10 bitcoins from Nakamoto in the world's first bitcoin transaction.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[86]</a></sup> Other early supporters were Wei Dai, creator of bitcoin predecessor <i>b-money</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Nick_Szabo" title="Nick Szabo">Nick Szabo</a>, creator of bitcoin predecessor <i><a href="/wiki/Bit_gold" class="mw-redirect" title="Bit gold">bit gold</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-WallaceWired11_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WallaceWired11-94">[87]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>In the early days, Nakamoto is estimated to have mined 1 million bitcoins.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup> Before disappearing from any involvement in bitcoin, Nakamoto in a sense handed over the reins to developer <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Andresen" title="Gavin Andresen">Gavin Andresen</a>, who then became the bitcoin lead developer at the <a href="/wiki/Bitcoin_Foundation" title="Bitcoin Foundation">Bitcoin Foundation</a>, the 'anarchic' bitcoin community's closest thing to an official public face.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[89]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>The value of the first bitcoin transactions were negotiated by individuals on the <i>bitcointalk</i> forums with one notable transaction of 10,000 BTC used to indirectly purchase two pizzas delivered by <a href="/wiki/Papa_John%27s_Pizza" title="Papa John's Pizza">Papa John's</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wired:RFB_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wired:RFB-88">[81]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>On 6 August 2010, a major <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" title="Vulnerability (computing)">vulnerability</a> in the bitcoin protocol was spotted. Transactions were not properly verified before they were included in the blockchain, which let users bypass bitcoin's economic restrictions and create an indefinite number of bitcoins.<sup id="cite_ref-Monetarism1_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monetarism1-97">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NIST1_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIST1-98">[91]</a></sup> On 15 August, the vulnerability was exploited; over 184 billion bitcoins were generated in a transaction, and sent to two addresses on the network. Within hours, the transaction was spotted and erased from the transaction log after the bug was fixed and the network forked to an updated version of the bitcoin protocol.<sup id="cite_ref-SourceForge_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SourceForge-99">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Monetarism1_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monetarism1-97">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NIST1_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIST1-98">[91]</a></sup></p> 
        </div>
        <h2 class="in-block section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)">
         <div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Economics" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h2>
        <div class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4"> 
         <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
          Main article: 
          <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_bitcoin" title="Economics of bitcoin">Economics of bitcoin</a>
         </div> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Classification" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Bitcoin is a <a href="/wiki/Digital_asset" title="Digital asset">digital asset</a><sup id="cite_ref-revealedcreator_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-revealedcreator-100">[93]</a></sup> designed by its inventor, Satoshi Nakamoto, to work as a currency.<sup id="cite_ref-paper_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-paper-24">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Monetarists_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monetarists-101">[94]</a></sup> It is commonly referred to with terms like digital currency,<sup id="cite_ref-primer_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-primer-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> <a href="/wiki/Digital_cash" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital cash">digital cash</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Virtual_currency" title="Virtual currency">virtual currency</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-satoshi_unit_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-satoshi_unit-13">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electronic_currency" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronic currency">electronic currency</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-capitalization_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capitalization-27">[23]</a></sup> or cryptocurrency.<sup id="cite_ref-currencydispute_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-currencydispute-103">[96]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>The question whether bitcoin is a <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> or not is still disputed.<sup id="cite_ref-currencydispute_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-currencydispute-103">[96]</a></sup> Bitcoins have three useful qualities in a currency, according to <i>The Economist</i> in January 2015: they are "hard to earn, limited in supply and easy to verify".<sup id="cite_ref-ec1305_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec1305-104">[97]</a></sup> Economists define money as a <a href="/wiki/Store_of_value" title="Store of value">store of value</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Medium_of_exchange" title="Medium of exchange">medium of exchange</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_account" title="Unit of account">unit of account</a> and agree that bitcoin has some way to go to meet all these criteria.<sup id="cite_ref-econ315_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ315-105">[98]</a></sup> It does best as a medium of exchange; as of February 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the number of merchants accepting bitcoin had passed 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-100tmerchants_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100tmerchants-22">[18]</a></sup> As of March 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the bitcoin market suffered from <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(finance)" title="Volatility (finance)">volatility</a>, limiting the ability of bitcoin to act as a stable store of value, and retailers accepting bitcoin use other currencies as their principal unit of account.<sup id="cite_ref-econ315_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econ315-105">[98]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="General_use">General use</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: General use" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg/220px-Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="319">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg/220px-Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg/330px-Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg/440px-Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Estimated-transaction-volume-usd.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Liquidity (estimated, USD/year, logarithmic scale).
            <sup id="cite_ref-Blockchain.info_35-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blockchain.info-35">[31]</a></sup>
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>According to research produced by <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> there were between 2.9 million and 5.8 million unique users using a cryptocurrency wallet, as of 2017, most of them using bitcoin. The number of users has grown significantly since 2013, when there were 300,000 to 1.3 million users.<sup id="cite_ref-CU2017_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CU2017-23">[19]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Acceptance_by_merchants">Acceptance by merchants</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Acceptance by merchants" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>In 2015, the number of merchants accepting bitcoin exceeded 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-100tmerchants_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100tmerchants-22">[18]</a></sup> Instead of 2–3% typically imposed by <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">credit card</a> processors, merchants accepting bitcoins often pay fees in the range from 0% to less than 2%.<sup id="cite_ref-merchantfee_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merchantfee-106">[99]</a></sup> Firms that accepted payments in bitcoin as of December 2014 included <a href="/wiki/PayPal" title="PayPal">PayPal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-msftaccepts_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msftaccepts-108">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dell" title="Dell">Dell</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Dell_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dell-109">[102]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Newegg" title="Newegg">Newegg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[103]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Payment_service_providers">Payment service providers</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Payment service providers" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Merchants accepting bitcoin ordinarily use the services of bitcoin payment service providers such as <a href="/wiki/BitPay" title="BitPay">BitPay</a> or <a href="/wiki/Coinbase" title="Coinbase">Coinbase</a>. When a customer pays in bitcoin, the payment service provider accepts the bitcoin on behalf of the merchant, converts it to the local currency, and sends the obtained amount to merchant's bank account, charging a fee for the service.<sup id="cite_ref-Reutersmerchants_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reutersmerchants-111">[104]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_institutions">Financial institutions</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Financial institutions" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Bitcoin companies have had difficulty opening traditional bank accounts because lenders have been leery of bitcoin's links to illicit activity.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[105]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Tony_Gallippi" title="Tony Gallippi">Antonio Gallippi</a>, a co-founder of <a href="/wiki/BitPay" title="BitPay">BitPay</a>, "banks are scared to deal with bitcoin companies, even if they really want to".<sup id="cite_ref-scaredbankers_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scaredbankers-113">[106]</a></sup> In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/National_Australia_Bank" title="National Australia Bank">National Australia Bank</a> closed accounts of businesses with ties to bitcoin,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/HSBC" title="HSBC">HSBC</a> refused to serve a hedge fund with links to bitcoin.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[108]</a></sup> Australian banks in general have been reported as closing down bank accounts of operators of businesses involving the currency;<sup id="cite_ref-afr.com_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afr.com-116">[109]</a></sup> this has become the subject of an investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Competition_and_Consumer_Commission" title="Australian Competition and Consumer Commission">Australian Competition and Consumer Commission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-afr.com_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-afr.com-116">[109]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Australian banks have keenly adopted the blockchain technology on which bitcoin is based.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>In a 2013 report, Bank of America Merrill Lynch stated that "we believe bitcoin can become a major means of payment for e-commerce and may emerge as a serious competitor to traditional money-transfer providers."<sup id="cite_ref-ForbesDec_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ForbesDec-118">[111]</a></sup> In June 2014, the first bank that converts deposits in currencies instantly to bitcoin without any fees was opened in Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="As_an_investment">As an investment</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: As an investment" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p>Some Argentinians have bought bitcoins to protect their savings against high inflation or the possibility that governments could confiscate savings accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-5facts_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5facts-70">[63]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%932013_Cypriot_financial_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2012–2013 Cypriot financial crisis">2012–2013 Cypriot financial crisis</a>, bitcoin purchases in Cyprus rose due to fears that savings accounts would be confiscated or taxed.<sup id="cite_ref-BloombergCyprus_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloombergCyprus-120">[113]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>The <a href="/wiki/Winklevoss_twins" title="Winklevoss twins">Winklevoss twins</a> have invested into bitcoins. In 2013 <i>The Washington Post</i> claimed that they owned 1% of all the bitcoins in existence at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[114]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>Other methods of investment are bitcoin funds. The first regulated bitcoin fund was established in Jersey in July 2014 and approved by the Jersey Financial Services Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-BitcoinJersey_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitcoinJersey-122">[115]</a></sup> Forbes started publishing arguments in favor of investing in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[116]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>In 2013 and 2014, the <a href="/wiki/European_Banking_Authority" title="European Banking Authority">European Banking Authority</a><sup id="cite_ref-ebawarn_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ebawarn-124">[117]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Industry_Regulatory_Authority" title="Financial Industry Regulatory Authority">Financial Industry Regulatory Authority</a> (FINRA), a United States <a href="/wiki/Self-regulatory_organization" title="Self-regulatory organization">self-regulatory organization</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-finrawarn_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finrawarn-125">[118]</a></sup> warned that investing in bitcoins carries significant risks. Forbes named bitcoin the best investment of 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[119]</a></sup> In 2014, Bloomberg named bitcoin one of its worst investments of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-worst_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worst-127">[120]</a></sup> In 2015, bitcoin topped Bloomberg's currency tables.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>According to <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_L.P." title="Bloomberg L.P.">Bloomberg</a>, in 2013 there were about 250 bitcoin wallets with more than $1 million worth of bitcoins. The number of bitcoin millionaires is uncertain as people can have more than one wallet.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Venture_capital">Venture capital</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Venture capital" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <p><a href="/wiki/Venture_capital" title="Venture capital">Venture capitalists</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_Thiel" title="Peter Thiel">Peter Thiel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Founders_Fund" title="Founders Fund">Founders Fund</a>, which invested <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>3 million in <a href="/wiki/BitPay" title="BitPay">BitPay</a>, do not purchase bitcoins themselves, instead funding bitcoin infrastructure like <a href="/wiki/List_of_bitcoin_companies" title="List of bitcoin companies">companies</a> that provide payment systems to merchants, exchanges, wallet services, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-mtr20130612_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mtr20130612-130">[123]</a></sup> In 2012, an incubator for bitcoin-focused start-ups was founded by Adam Draper, with financing help from his father, venture capitalist <a href="/wiki/Timothy_C._Draper" class="mw-redirect" title="Timothy C. Draper">Tim Draper</a>, one of the largest bitcoin holders after winning an auction of 30,000 bitcoins,<sup id="cite_ref-wsj1214_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj1214-131">[124]</a></sup> at the time called 'mystery buyer'.<sup id="cite_ref-guard714_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guard714-132">[125]</a></sup> The company's goal is to fund 100 bitcoin businesses within 2–3 years with $10,000 to $20,000 for a 6% stake.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj1214_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj1214-131">[124]</a></sup> Investors also invest in bitcoin mining.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[126]</a></sup> According to a 2015 study by <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Tasca" title="Paolo Tasca">Paolo Tasca</a>, bitcoin startups raised almost $1 billion in three years (Q1 2012 – Q1 2015).<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup></p> 
         <h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Price_and_volatility">Price and volatility</span><span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Price and volatility" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-edit-enabled edit-page">Edit</a></span></h3> 
         <div class="thumb tright"> 
          <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg" class="image">
            <noscript>
             <img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg/220px-Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="319">
            </noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 124px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg/220px-Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="124" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg/330px-Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg/440px-Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage">&nbsp;</span></a> 
           <div class="thumbcaption"> 
            <div class="magnify">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Bitcoin_price_and_volatility.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
            </div> Price
            <sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[note 7]</a></sup> (left vertical axis, logarithmic scale) and volatility
            <sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[note 8]</a></sup> (right vertical axis).
            <sup id="cite_ref-Blockchain.info_35-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blockchain.info-35">[31]</a></sup>
           </div> 
          </div> 
         </div> 
         <p>According to <a href="/wiki/Mark_T._Williams" title="Mark T. Williams">Mark T. Williams</a>, as of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, bitcoin has <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(finance)" title="Volatility (finance)">volatility</a> seven times greater than gold, eight times greater than the <a href="/wiki/S%26P_500" class="mw-redirect" title="S&amp;P 500">S&amp;P 500</a>, and 18 times greater than the U.S. dollar.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[128]</a></sup> According to <i>Forbes</i>, there are uses where volatility does not matter, such as online gambling, tipping, and international remittances.<sup id="cite_ref-VolatilityFatal_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VolatilityFatal-138">[129]</a></sup></p> 
         <p>The price of bitcoins has gone through various cycles of appreciation and depreciation referred to by some as <a href="/wiki/Economic_bubble" title="Economic bubble">bubbles</a> and busts.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup> In 2011, the value of one bitcoin rapidly rose from about US$0.30 to US$32 before returning to US$2.<sup id="cite_ref-Leebubble_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Leebubble-141">[132]</a></sup> In the latter half of 2012 and during the <a href="/wiki/2012%E2%80%9313_Cypriot_financial_crisis" title="2012–13 Cypriot financial crisis">2012–13 Cypriot financial crisis</a>, the bitcoin price began to rise,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[133]</a></sup> reaching a high of US$266 on 10 April 2013, before crashing to around US$50.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[134]</a></sup> On 29 November 2013, the cost of one bitcoin rose to a peak of US$1,242.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[135]</a></sup> In 2014, the price fell sharply, and as of April remained depressed at little more than half 2013 prices. As of August 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bitcoin&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> it was under US$600.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[136]</a></sup> In January 2015, noting that the bitcoin price had dropped to its lowest level since spring 2013 – around US$224 – <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> suggested that "[w]ith no signs of a rally in the offing, the industry is bracing for the effects of a prolonged decline in prices.
